					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Undergraduate Program Handbook</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-243" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><em><strong>Contents: </strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href="#requirements" rel="nofollow">requirements</a>&nbsp;/&nbsp;<a href="#advising" rel="nofollow">advising</a>&nbsp;/&nbsp;<a href="#honors" rel="nofollow">honors</a>&nbsp;/<a href="#questions" rel="nofollow">&nbsp;frequent questions</a>/</em><br /><em>__________________________________________________</em></p>
<p>The undergraduate program in Literary Arts at Brown is designed to allow student writers to develop their skills in one or more genres, while deepening their understanding of the craft of writing. Concentration requirements are intended to balance creative and critical concerns.&nbsp;</p>
<p>By the time they graduate, concentrators should possess critical acumen, attain fluency in writing in general and within one or more genres as well. The department also wants our concentrators to learn something about an art: to gain a sense of satisfaction and accomplishment in developing their craft and sensibility as artists.</p>
<h3><strong><a name="requirements"></a>Concentration Requirements</strong></h3>
<p>Concentrators must take four workshop courses from among LITR 110, 210, 310, 610, 1010, 1110, 1150, 1310 and 1510. At the discretion of the concentration advisor, workshop credit may also be given for Playwriting workshops given by the departments of Africana Studies and Theatre and Performance Studies; credit may also be given for 1000-level writing courses given through the English Department.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Concentrators must also take six literature courses*, including: a course in literary theory or history of literary criticism; two courses that cover reading and research in literary arts created before the 20th century (these must cover two distinct time periods); and one course that covers literary arts created after the 19th century.</p>
<p>During the senior year, all concentrators must take one course from among the Literary Arts offerings (designated as LITR or cross-listed by LITR).&nbsp;</p>
<p>At least four courses counted toward the concentration must be at the 1000-level.&nbsp;</p>
<p>At least six courses counted toward the concentration must be taken in the Literary Arts Department (with the approval of the concentration advisor, the rest may be taken at other institutions or other departments at Brown that offer literature courses).</p>
<p>No more than two courses may count toward the Literary Arts concentration and toward a second concentration.</p>
<p>*By literature courses, the department means courses where the primary readings for the course are works of literature.</p>
<h3><a name="advising"></a>Concentration Advising</h3>
<p>To learn more about the concentration and before you file your concentration forms online, we recommend that you make an appointment with Gale Nelson -- contact him by electronic mail at <a href="mailto:Gale_Nelson@brown.edu">Gale_Nelson@brown.edu</a>. Gale Nelson's office is Room 109, Literary Arts Building, 68 1/2 Brown Street.&nbsp;</p>
<p>To establish your Literary Arts concentration, you'll need to visit the Registrars website, where you can complete the concentration filing process online.</p>
<h3><a name="honors"></a>Honors in Creative Writing</h3>
<p>Literary Arts concentrators who wish to complete their concentration with Honors in Creative Writing must include two 1000-level workshops or independent studies among their courses and must, in addition, complete a thesis. The thesis is a substantial body of work that reflects the student’s achievement during his/her years in the Department: a collection of stories or poems, a novel, a play, screenplay, a literary translation, a cross-disciplinary project or an extended electronic/mixed media project. Students may complete a thesis by taking either LITR 1510 (an independent study) or by taking LITR 1410 (the Honors workshop).</p>
<p>Students wishing to complete Honors in Cross-Disciplinary writing must take either two special topics workshops, one special topics plus one genre workshop, or one workshop in each of two different genres.</p>
<p>Applications to the Honors Program are due between the first day of the fall semester and 20 September (or the first Monday thereafter if the 20th falls on a weekend). Applications are considered annually. In order to apply, candidates must have taken OR be taking a workshop in the respective genre at the 1000-level (the workshop may be LITR 1010, 1110 or 1150; LITR 1310 also makes one eligible). Candidates may apply in more than one genre (if eligible), but will do ONE thesis (with the thesis advisor’s consent, a thesis may cross the boundaries of genre).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Theses are typically completed in the senior year, and are due on 13 April (Samuel Beckett’s birthday) or the Monday thereafter when the 13th falls on a weekend). If a concentrator is to complete course work in December, the thesis may be completed by the previous 13 April, by the end of December, or by the following 13 April (the date selected must be agreed upon by the thesis advisor). Most students complete the thesis through an honors independent study or through an honors workshop.</p>
<h3><a name="questions"></a>Frequent Questions</h3>
<p><strong><em>Do I have to complete a thesis for a Literary Arts concentration?</em></strong> &nbsp;No; but you must complete a thesis to be considered for honors.</p>
<p><strong><em>If I don’t complete a thesis, what do I do in my senior year?</em></strong> <br />All concentrators must complete at least one course through the Literary Arts program in the senior year. In many cases, this will be a literature seminar or a workshop. This course constitutes the capstone experience in Literary Arts.</p>
<p><strong><em>Do many concentrators pursue a second concentration? <br /></em></strong>We’ve found that about a third of the Literary Arts concentrators complete two concentrations. The “other” concentrations have been spread widely throughout the college – there’s no particular pattern to this. If you have two strong interests, and feel that you can comfortably undertake two concentrations while also having a rich and diverse educational experience, we recommend that you consider this.</p>
<p><strong><em>Do concentrators have advantages over other students in terms of getting into Literary Arts classes?</em></strong> <br />During the registration period, we make ten slots available to concentrators in LITR 210 sections. By registering during that period, concentrators are thereby not required to submit writing samples for these intermediate writing sections. Certain literature courses offered though Literary Arts may also allow concentrators to register without instructor permission (override) during the registration period.</p>

				<div id="content">
					<div class="marginator">
					
					

									
					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Applying for Honors</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-190" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>If you are a Literary Arts concentrator who has completed or is currently enrolled in a 1000-level Literary Arts Workshop or an appropriate LITR 1110 or 11150 section, you are eligible to apply for Honors in Literary Arts.</p>
<p>To apply, you should complete an online application in the Fall semester, typically of the academic year in which the thesis will be completed (thesis work is completed in the Spring semester).&nbsp; Applications are due between Sept. 8 and Sept. 25 at 11:59 pm.</p>
<p>The Honors application will ask a few questions – your name, your class year, your Banner number, etc.&nbsp; There is also a place to upload a writing sample.&nbsp; You should upload a writing sample in the genre in which you want to write a thesis.&nbsp; The sample should be no less than 20 pages of material.&nbsp; Please note the sample should be a single file, uploaded as a PDF.&nbsp; (If you want to submit eight really short stories, or 15 poems, please combine them into a single file and send them as one document, even though there are various works under the one umbrella).</p>
<p>Once you have submitted the application, the faculty in your genre who will be available to work with you on your thesis will review it.&nbsp; This review is done independently – each available faculty member will separately review each application, and will submit decisions to the department.&nbsp; You may, therefore, be accepted into the Honors program by any faculty member within your genre; and by accepting you, the faculty member is agreeing to serve as your advisor (typically through an independent study conducted in the spring of your senior year, but sometimes through an Advanced or Honors workshop).</p>
<p>Once you are accepted into the Honors program, you should approach your faculty advisor directly to begin making plans.</p>
<p>Please note:</p>
<p>1.&nbsp; You should not approach faculty members and ask them to serve as your advisor before you complete this application process.&nbsp; This process is designed to ensure that all applicants are given equal consideration, so that the process is fair to everyone.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>2.&nbsp; It is possible to apply for Honors in your junior year; if accepted, you will complete your Honors thesis in the spring of your senior year.&nbsp; Most students are accepted into Honors, however, in the fall of their senior year.</p>
<p>3.&nbsp; Decisions regarding Honors will be announced in mid-October.&nbsp; You will be notified via campus mail.</p>
  </div>

	
  <div class="clear-block">
    <div class="meta">

			
	    
		</div><!-- end .meta -->
	</div>

</article>
							</div>

	<div id="in-literary-arts---" class="clear-block block block-calwidget ">
	
	<h2>In Literary Arts...</h2>	
		<div class="content"><section class="events-list">
      <div class="date">
        <h5><span class="month day-of-the-week">
          Oct        </span>
        <span class="day">
          18        </span></h5>

<!-- Layout -->

				<div id="content">
					<div class="marginator">
					
					

									
					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Information for Current Honors Students</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-186" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>This material is intended for those advanced undergraduate who have been accepted into the honors/capstone program. As you will be expected to complete a book-length creative manuscript before graduating, we wanted to give you some advice at the outset of this process.</p>
<p>Your advisor will play an important role in this process. You should approach that person as soon as you can, and begin to plan how you are going to complete your thesis/project. The advisor selected you as someone s/he is willing to advise; however, if you want to work with another member of the literary arts faculty, you may approach that person. It is up to the faculty members themselves to agree to take you on. It is the obligation of the instructor to say, "I'm sorry, but I cannot work with you" if s/he is already overloaded with advisees.</p>
<p>A graduate student may not serve as advisor.</p>
<p>Once you have settled on an advisor, you should complete the "Student Advisor Form" (a copy of which will be provided to you upon being accepted into the program).</p>
<p>You may want to begin work on the project as soon as possible. With your advisor's permission, you may sign up for an independent study for your seventh semester (LITR 1310) and/or for your final semester (LITR 1510). You may only use LITR 1510 in your final semester.</p>
<p>You may also be interested in taking one of the LITR 1410 honors/capstone workshop sections, to be offered in the spring semester. Entry into these sections is at the discretion of the instructors.</p>
<p>You should expect to meet with your advisor at regular intervals to discuss your progress. Different projects require different types of advising at different intervals. You should work with your advisor to work out what would be most appropriate. In most cases, writers have found that meeting with an advisor about twice a month is appropriate. Meetings may last as little as 15 minutes and as long as an hour-but usually not for a set length of time.</p>
<p>You may find that there are periods in the process where you need different types of help-where a suggested reading or exercise may be more necessary than a close reading. It will be up to both you and your advisor to be aware of what the project needs.</p>
<p>Some writers find it useful to set up a time line, working backwards from the date the project is due. Again, this is more useful for some writers (and some projects) than others. Not everyone thinks in terms of self-imposed deadlines, but you need to be reminded that the final deadline is for a finished work, not a draft. You submit two copies of your project to the program office on the date when projects are due no later than 4 p.m.</p>
<p>Once you turn in your project, your advisor will have a limited amount of time to read your work and decide whether or not it meets the program's standards for honors or capstone certification. Your advisor will write up a paragraph recommendation, which will be submitted to the program, and then, to the college. You will also get a copy of your advisor's recommendation.</p>
<p>Once you've received your advisor's comments, we encourage you to set up a meeting to discuss those comments. If you have specific questions, you should ask at that time.</p>
<p>To end each academic year, the program sponsors the Honors/Capstone reading series. You'll be notified of the dates available for sign-up. This marks the primary celebration of your long and hard work. We hope that you find the event festive-and we encourage you to attend the readings of your colleagues.</p>
<p>If you have any questions along the way, please discuss them with your advisor or with Gale Nelson, room 109.</p>
<p><strong>Second Readers</strong></p>
<p>Many candidates find it useful to have a second reader. You should feel free to approach a member of the faculty to seek input in this capacity. This may be especially useful if you intend to cross the boundaries of genre in your project; or if you have worked closely with two different faculty members, and know that both would provide valuable insights into your project.</p>
<p>For administrative purposes, the program will only record and track your first reader.</p>
<p><strong><br /></strong></p>

					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Formatting and Presenting Your Thesis</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-189" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>Students present the projects in final form. You should show your advisor a draft of the work well before the deadline to ensure s/he will have time to review it and give suggestions on a timely basis. You should make arrangements with your advisor as to how you will provide her/him with the final version. Submit two copies of your project to the program office by 4 pm on or before 13 April.</p>
<p class="alpha-upper"><strong>Manuscript Guidelines&nbsp;</strong></p>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Type and Spacing:</em> 10, 11 or 12 point typeface (for typewriters, pica and elite are both accepted). Prose manuscripts are usually double-spaced. Poetry and plays are as often single-spaced as double-spaced.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Margins:</em> the left margin should be at least 1-1/2 inches wide (to accommodate the binding); the top, bottom and right margins are typically at least 1 inch wide.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Page numbers:</em> page numbers may be placed closer to the edge of the paper than the text itself. It is recommended that page numbers be placed in the same location throughout the manuscript.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Length:</em> most projects will be no less than 50 pages.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Binding:</em> The archive copy of the manuscript must be bound: vello-binding and comb binding are typical (and available through most copy or office supply stores). A big staple, an elastic or a paper clip are not appropriate; honors or capstone certification will not be awarded until the program has received an appropriately-bound manuscript.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Electronic manuscripts:</em> along with a web site address or disk, the program requires that a paper version of the final project be submitted for archival purposes. Disks and web addresses may, some day, be replaced by new technologies-as such, the paper version may eventually be the only surviving document.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>Signature page and authorization to lend and reproduce the manuscript:</em> This page should not be bound into the project, but is required. You may NOT re-word the material on this page (it should not be considered a part of the project itself; as such, it should not be thought of as a place to be "creative"). A sample of the signature page is found in below in <em>Related Files</em>.</div>
<div class="content" style="margin-top:0.6em; margin-right:0px; margin-bottom:0.6em; margin-left:0px; color:#2f2a20; padding:0px"><em>&nbsp;</em><em>&nbsp;</em><em>Title page:</em> the title, your name and the date of submission must be included. We provide a sample title page, but as long as the information noted is included, you may format this in any way you like. &nbsp;A sample of the title page is found in below in&nbsp;<em>Related Files</em>.</div>
<fieldset class="fieldgroup group-related-files"><legend>Related Files</legend><div class="field field-type-filefield field-field-related-files">
    <div class="field-items">
            <div class="field-item odd">
                    <div class="filefield-file"><img class="filefield-icon field-icon-application-pdf"  alt="application/pdf icon" src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/sites/all/modules/filefield/icons/application-pdf.png" /><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/sites/brown.edu.academics.literary-arts/files/uploads/Honors%20Thesis%20Signature%20Page.pdf" type="application/pdf; length=35901">Honors Thesis Signature Page.pdf</a></div>        </div>
              <div class="field-item even">
                    <div class="filefield-file"><img class="filefield-icon field-icon-application-vnd-openxmlformats-officedocument-wordprocessingml-document"  alt="application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.wordprocessingml.document icon" src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/sites/all/modules/filefield/icons/x-office-document.png" /><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/sites/brown.edu.academics.literary-arts/files/uploads/Sample%20Title%20Page.docx" type="application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.wordprocessingml.document; length=33088" title="Sample Title Page.docx">Sample Title Page</a></div>        </div>

  </div>

	</div>

<p><strong><span style="text-decoration:underline">25 September by 4 pm.</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>To apply, click <a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/application-honors-creative-writing">Honors application</a>.</strong></p>
<p><span>The review process takes 3-4 weeks. &nbsp;Notification will be made via campus mail.</span></p>

					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Honors:  How to Complete Your Project</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-187" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2>Four Ways to Complete Your Project</h2>
<p>During your final semester at Brown, there are four ways for you to complete your project:</p>
<p>1. Take an advanced workshop (LITR 1010, LITR 1110 or LITR 1150), taught by the instructor advising your project.</p>
<p>2. Take one of the Honors/Capstone Workshops (LITR 1410); this option is available in spring only.</p>
<p>3. Undertake an independent study in Creative Writing (LITR 1510).</p>
<p>4. &nbsp;Work independently on your project, but receive no course credit for the project. To undertake this option, you must receive permission from your advisor.</p>
<p>You must select one from among these choices; you may not get course credit for a workshop and an independent study for the same project in one semester. You may, however, complete an independent study in the fall and then undertake LITR 1410 in the spring (with permission of the instructors).</p>
<p><strong>LITR 1010, LITR 1110 &amp; LITR 1150 Advanced Level Workshops</strong></p>
<p>If your advisor is teaching an advanced-level workshop, s/he may encourage you to take the workshop to provide a forum for you to undertake the completion of your project. Admission to the workshop is based on a writing sample, due on the first day of the semester.</p>
<p><strong>LITR 1410 Honors/Capstone Workshops</strong></p>
<p>These workshops are intended as forums for students working on their theses and Capstone projects in creative writing. In consultation with your advisor and the respective workshop leader, you may select to take the workshop appropriate for the genre or an independent study, but cannot choose to do both during the same semester. Instructors of these spring semester workshops (unlike all other undergraduate workshops) will give permission to candidates during the fall semester.</p>
<p>If you are interested in taking one of these sections, you should approach the appropriate instructor during the registration period in the fall semester.</p>
<p><strong>LITR 1310 Independent Study in Creative Writing</strong></p>
<p>This course is typically taken by Honors/Capstone candidates in their penultimate semester. The instructor should meet with each student at regular intervals (at least every other week); the student should complete a substantial amount of reading and writing over the course of the semester.</p>
<p><strong>LITR 1510 Honors/Capstone Ind. Study in Creative Writing</strong></p>
<p>This course is typically taken by Honors/Capstone candidates in their final semester. The instructor should meet with each student at regular intervals (at least every other week); the student should complete a substantial amount of reading and writing over the course of the semester.</p>
<div></div>
<p><span style="color:#2f2a20">&nbsp;</span></p>
<div></div>
  </div>

<!-- Layout -->

				<div id="content">
					<div class="marginator">
					
					

									
					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Important Deadlines</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-188" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>Submit Advisor Form to main office at 68 1/2 Brown Street by the date noted on the form. Generally this timeframe starts the first day of classes to the third week of September.</p>
<p>On 13 April, present final copy of the project to advisor (if 13 April falls on a weekend or holiday, the thesis is due on the last business day before the 13th); so that we may submit the necessary report to the College on time, the program will expect reports from the faculty reviewers soon after the 13 April deadline. As such, there is little to no room for a deadline extension.</p>
<p>Note: whether you finish your course work in the fall or spring semester, the thesis is due on or before 13 April. If you are graduating in the preceding fall semester and wish to submit your thesis in the fall semester, you need to make arrangements with your advisor directly.</p>
<div></div>
<p><span style="color:#2f2a20">&nbsp;</span></p>
<div></div>
  </div>

	
  <div class="clear-block">
    <div class="meta">

			
	    
		</div><!-- end .meta -->
	</div>

</article>

				<div id="content">
					<div class="marginator">
					
					

									
					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Undergraduate Program</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-184" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>The Department in Literary Arts offers courses in fiction, poetry, screenwriting, literary hypermedia, and translation, as well as seminar courses in reading and writing about literature. &nbsp;Students may choose to focus in a single genre, or may opt to work in two or more genres. &nbsp;For some students, cross-disciplinary work may incorporate music, visual art, or performance art. &nbsp;Department faculty encourage students to take workshops in various genres, and classes in other art forms in addition to writing.</p>
<p>Types of classes offered by the Literary Arts Department include:</p>
<p><strong>Workshops in Creative Writing I: </strong>&nbsp;These workshops are designed for students who have little or no previous experience writing literary texts in a particular genre. &nbsp;Introductory workshops introduce students to a variety of technical and imaginative concerns through exercises, readings, and discussions.</p>
<p><strong>Workshops in Creative Writing II:</strong> &nbsp;These workshops are intended for students who have some experience in writing literary texts. &nbsp;Most sections require readings from published work in addition to writing exercises and workshop discussions of works-in-progress.</p>
<p><strong>Advanced Creative Writing Workshops:</strong> &nbsp;These classes provide students with a forum for extended practice in the art of creative writing. &nbsp;Most sections require extensive reading assignments as well as the writing and workshop discussion of significant projects in the genre.</p>
<p><strong>Advanced Creative Writing Special Topics Workshops: </strong>&nbsp;Special topics workshops also provide a forum for extended practice of the art of writing, but within the context of a special topic that may focus on a particular genre or cross the boundaries of the genre. &nbsp;Some recent special topics have included American Political Drama, Electronic Writing, The Short Story, Innovative Narrative, The Novel in a Multicultural Context, and Plasticity in Playwriting.</p>
<p><strong>Courses in Reading, Research and Writing About Literature: </strong>&nbsp;These are lecture and seminar courses designed for students who have an interest in reading, researching and writing about literature. &nbsp;Some recent offerings have included Writing the World; Samuel Beckett; and History and Practice of English Versification; and Writers on Writing.</p>
<p><strong>Independent Study in Creative Writing: </strong>&nbsp;These courses offer tutorial instruction oriented toward a significant work-in-progress by the student. &nbsp;Independent Studies are typically taken by Honors candidates in their final or penultimate semester.</p>
<p><strong>Honors Workshops in Creative Writing: </strong>&nbsp;These workshops provide a forum for students who are working on thesis projects. &nbsp;Significantly reading and writing are required.</p>
<div></div>
<p><a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/literary-arts/courses">Literary Arts Courses</a></p>

					<div class="columns main-columns">

						<h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Honors in Creative Writing</h1>

						

						<div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

																												
							
							<div class="clear-block">
								
<article id="node-185" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>Literary Arts concentrators who wish to complete their concentration with Honors in Creative Writing must include two 1000-level workshops or independent studies among their courses and must, in addition, complete a thesis. &nbsp;The thesis is a substantial body of work that reflects the student’s achievement during his/her years in the Program: &nbsp;a collection of stories or poems, a novel, a play or screenplay, or an extended electronic/mixed media project. &nbsp;Students may complete a thesis by taking either LR 1510 (an independent study) or by taking LR 1410 (the Honors workshop).</p>
<p>*Students wishing to complete Honors in Cross-Disciplinary writing must take either two special topics workshops, one special topics plus one genre workshop, or one workshop in each of two different genres.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<div></div>
  </div>

	
  <div class="clear-block">
    <div class="meta">

			
	    
		</div><!-- end .meta -->
	</div>

</article>
							</div>
